Objection and Answer on Universal Judgments of Wahm
Addresses the objection that wahm only perceives particulars, not universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the soul uses the imagination (wahm) to make universal judgments, and why such judgments are valid only when affirmed by reason.

Core Idea
The imagination is an instrument of the soul; reason validates its universal judgments.

This passage tackles a crucial objection in Islamic epistemology: if wahm only perceives particulars, how can it produce universal statements like 'every body is in a direction'? The answer reveals that wahm is not the true agent; the soul uses it as an instrument. By reading this, you will see how the imagination serves as a tool for the rational soul, and how reason ensures the validity of such judgments.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the core answer
The objection states that wahm perceives only particulars. The answer shifts the agent to the soul (al-nafs), which uses wahm as an instrument. Note the key shift in subject.
The soul is the actual judge and perceiver.
2
Understand the role of reason
The answer says wahm's judgment is valid only if reason (al-aql) witnesses it. The commentary explains two modes of affirmation: agreement or subsumption.
This ties imaginal judgments to a rational framework.
3
Consider the broader context of intuitive judgments
The passage ends by identifying these as complete intuitive judgments (al-hadsiyyat al-tammah) and notes al-Razi's view that such intuition does not require repeated observation.
This connects to higher epistemic categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (نحو كل جسم في جهة) فإن قلت: الوهم لا يدرك إلا المعاني الجزئية، فكيف يحكم حكماً كلياً؟ قلت: الحاكم والمدرك هو النفس والوهم آلة لها كالعقل، إلا أن الوهم القوى شديد العلاقة بالنفس تستعمله في غير المحسوسات أيضاً، فإن شاهده العقل كان وإلا فلا.
قوله: (فإن العقل يصدقه) أي في الجملة على ما هو الأصل في القضايا المطلقة عن الجهة، وتصديقه إما بأن يتفقا على ذلك الحكم كما في مثال المتن، أو يكون حكم الوهم مندرجاً في حكمه كما في قولنا هذا الجسم لا يكون في مكانين فإنه مندرج في قولنا الجسم الواحد لا يكون في مكانين. المخالفة والتحقيق أن ما ذكر هاهنا هو الحدسيات التامة، وقد تؤخذ على إطلاقها بحيث يتناول الحدس لصاحب القوة القدسية ومن يقربه، وإلى هذا ينظر قول الرازي في شرح الرسالة فإنه لا يحتاج إلى تكرار المشاهدة.
He said: (Such as 'every body is in a direction') For if you say: The imagination perceives only particular meanings, so how does it make a universal judgment? I say: The real judge and perceiver is the soul, and the imagination is an instrument for it, like the intellect, except that the imagination, being a strong faculty closely connected to the soul, employs it also in non-sensory matters. If the intellect witnesses it, it is [valid]; otherwise, not. He said: (For reason affirms it) i.e., in general, as is the principle in propositions that are absolute with respect to modality, and its affirmation is either by their agreeing on that judgment as in the example of the text, or by the judgment of the imagination being subsumed under its judgment, as in our saying 'this body is not in two places', for it is subsumed under our saying 'a single body is not in two places'. The opposition and verification is that what is mentioned here is the complete intuitive judgments, and they may be taken in an absolute sense so as to include the intuition of one possessing the holy power and those close to him; and towards this points the statement of al-Razi in his commentary on the Epistle, for he does not need repetition of observation.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the universality of imaginal judgments. The objection states that the imagination (al-wahm) only perceives particular meanings, so how can it make a universal judgment like 'every body is in a direction'? The answer explains that the true perceiver and judge is the soul (al-nafs); the imagination is merely an instrument for the soul, like the intellect. The imagination, because it is a strong power with a close connection to the soul, is used even in non-sensory matters. However, its judgment is valid only if reason witnesses it. The commentary further clarifies that reason's affirmation occurs either by both agreeing on the same judgment, or by the imaginal judgment being subsumed under a rational one. Finally, it notes that what is discussed here pertains to complete intuitive judgments (al-hadsiyyat al-tammah), and refers to al-Razi's view that such intuition does not require repeated observation.

Correction: The imagination can produce universal judgments because it is an instrument of the soul; the soul is the true perceiver and judge, and reason validates the judgment.

Checkpoint

A) The imagination itself.
B) The soul (al-nafs).
C) The senses.
D) The physical brain.

Key Takeaway
The imagination can produce universal judgments because it is an instrument of the soul, and its judgments are validated by reason.

The Seven Certain Premises: Definitions and First Principles
Sanusi introduces the seven certain premises and emphasizes the role of awwaliyat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven types of certain premises in Islamic theology, their hierarchy, and the special status of awwaliyat as the core.

Core Idea
Certain premises are ranked; awwaliyat are the bedrock of knowledge.

Sanusi begins by asserting that the foundational premises of kalam are extremely clear and rarely disputed, unlike judgments about abstract concepts. He then identifies awwaliyat (self-evident truths) as the core, explaining that only those with deficient instinct or corrupted disposition reject them. This lesson unpacks the seven categories and their logical relationships.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Familiarize yourself with the overall flow and the list of seven premises.
Pay attention to the commentary sections that clarify each term.
2
Identify the seven types and their order
List the seven categories as they appear: awwaliyat, qadaya fitriyya, mushahadat, wahmiyyat, mujarrabat, hadsiyyat, mutawatirat. Note the order and reasons.
The passage places awwaliyat first, then fitriyya, etc.
3
Understand the special status of awwaliyat
The text states awwaliyat are the core (al-umda) because only those with deficient instinct or corrupted fitra reject them. Grasp why they are foundational.
Awwaliyat are self-evident; no further reasoning needed.
4
Analyze conditions for proof
Notice that the last three types (mujarrabat, hadsiyyat, mutawatirat) are proof for oneself but not for others unless they share the requisite conditions. Reflect on this epistemic limitation.
This highlights the importance of shared experience.
5
Reflect on the inductive enumeration rationale
The passage explains that if mental representation of extremes suffices for judgment, it is awwaliyat; otherwise, other types are needed. Consider how this classification is exhaustive.
This rationale ties the seven together logically.

المرصد السادس - المقصد السادس : في المقدمات هي شديدة الوضوح، لا يكاد يقع فيها اختلاف الآراء كما وقع في غيرها بخلاف حكمه في المجردات والمعقولات الصرفة فإنه إذا حكم عليها بأحكام المحسوسات كان حكمه هناك حكم الأوليات كما مر . قوله : (ثم المشاهدات) أي قسم منها وهي المحسوسات وهي إنما تكون حجة على الغير، إذا شاركه في المشعر والشعور، وكذا الوهميات ولم يقيدهما بذلك لظهوره، وإنما كانت بعد القضايا الفطرية لكونها أحكاماً جزئية لا تفاوت بينهما في القطعية . قوله : (ثم الوهميات لكون مدركها قوة باطنة محتاجة إلى شهادة العقل بها. قوله : ( بأن يقنع من الإقناع بمعنى الإرضاء والمناكرة المقابلة والمحاربة متعلق بقوله جاحدها أي لا يمكن إرضاء جاحد الأقسام الثلاثة، إذا كان جحوده على سبيل المخاصمة، والمحاربة بخلاف ما إذا كان جحوده على سبيل الاستفادة فإنه يمكن إرضاؤه إذا اعترف بالاشتراك فيما يقتضيها . المحسوسات مطلقاً من قبيل الضروريات كما يدل عليه السياق، وإطلاقاتهم أيضاً خطأ لأنها وإن تعلقت بالمحسوس فربما تغلط كتوهم صداقة من ليس له هي . قوله : (ثم المشاهدات) أي نوع منها فقط، وهو الذي يستند إلى الحس الظاهر لأن الوجدانيات نوع آخر منها، وليست عمدة أصلاً كما مر في المرصد الرابع في إثبات العلوم الضرورية، ثم شرط المشاركة لا بد في المشاهدات أيضاً على ما مر في ذلك المرصد، قيل: لعل عدم ذكره ها هنا لأن معظم المشاهدات مثل وجود السماء والأرض وغير ذلك مما تبتني عليه المسائل الكلامية مشترك بين الكل، وفيه ما فيه هذا وقد نبهت هناك على ما بين كلاميه في ذلك المقصد وهذا المرصد من المخالفة فليتذكر.
The sixth observation - the sixth purpose: On premises. They are extremely clear, hardly any disagreement occurs in them as occurred in others, unlike his judgment about abstract and pure intelligibles, for if he judges them by the judgments of sensibles, his judgment there is false, like his judgment that every existent must be in a direction and in a place. And know that the core of these first principles are the seven awwaliyat, for none halt at them except one of deficient instinct like imbeciles and children, or one whose innate disposition is polluted by beliefs contrary to awwaliyat, as with some of the ignorant and commoners. Then the qadaya fitriyya [intuitive propositions] with their syllogism, then mushahadat [observations], then wahmiyyat [imaginations]. As for mujarrabat [experiential], hadsiyyat [intuitive], and mutawatirat [recurrent reports], they are proof for a person concerning himself but not proof for him against others unless the other shares in the matters that necessitate them, such as experience, intuition, and recurrence. So one cannot satisfy one who denies them in the manner of stubborn opposition. And the reason for the inductive enumeration in these seven is that if the mental representation of the two extremes suffices for the judgment of the intellect, it is awwaliyat; if not, [then...] His statement: '(and pure intelligibles)' even if they are not specific to abstracts. His statement: '(by the judgments of sensibles)' meaning by judgments specific to sensibles. His statement: '(the core)' meaning in terms of being proof for oneself and also for others. His statement: '(then the qadaya [etc.])' because they are in the category of awwaliyat as mentioned. His statement: '(then mushahadat)' meaning a part of them, namely sensibles, and they are proof against others only if the other shares in the sense and sensation, and likewise wahmiyyat, and he did not restrict them by that because it is obvious. They are placed after qadaya fitriyya because they are particular judgments, with no difference between them in certainty. His statement: '(then wahmiyyat because their perceiver is an internal power needing the testimony of the intellect to them).' His statement: '(so one cannot satisfy)' from 'iqna' meaning satisfaction, and 'munakara' meaning confrontation and combat, related to his statement 'one who denies' meaning: one cannot satisfy the denier of the three categories if his denial is in the manner of quarreling and combat, unlike if his denial is in the manner of seeking benefit, for then he can be satisfied if he acknowledges sharing in what necessitates them. Sensibles absolutely are of the type of necessities as the context indicates, and their unrestricted use is also wrong because although they relate to the sensible, they may err, like imagining friendship from one who is not. His statement: '(then mushahadat)' meaning only one type thereof, which is based on external sense, because inner feelings are another type and are not fundamental at all, as mentioned in the fourth observation on proving necessary sciences. Then the condition of participation is necessary in mushahadat as well, according to what was mentioned in that observation. It is said: perhaps he did not mention it here because most mushahadat, like the existence of the sky and earth and so forth on which kalam issues are based, are common to all. There is something in this, and I have noted there the discrepancy between his statements in that observation and this observation, so let it be remembered.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage defines the seven certain premises (al-muqaddimat al-yaqiniyya) and explains the special status of first principles (awwaliyat). The main text states that these premises are extremely clear (shadidat al-wuduh) and hardly subject to disagreement, unlike judgments about abstract intelligibles when one applies sensible categories to them. The core (al-umda) among these first principles are the awwaliyat, because only those with deficient instinct (naqis al-ghariza) or those whose innate disposition is corrupted by contrary beliefs (mudannis al-fitrat bi al-aqa'id al-mudadda li al-awwaliyat) would hesitate in them. The text then lists the seven types: awwaliyat, then qadaya fitriyya (intuitive propositions that come with their syllogism), mushahadat (observations), wahmiyyat (imaginations), mujarrabat (experiential), hadsiyyat (intuitive), and mutawatirat (recurrent reports). It explains that the last three are proof for the individual himself but not for others unless those others share in the requiite conditions (experience, intuition, recurrence). The gloss clarifies each term. For example, 'awwaliyat' are those where the mental representation of the two extremes suffices for judgment; if not, the intellect needs something else. The gloss on 'al-umda' specifies that awwaliyat are fundamental as proofs for oneself and others. The gloss on 'qadaya fitriyya' explains they are like awwaliyat in certainty. The gloss on 'mushahadat' notes they are a type of sensibles and require shared sense to be proof against others. The gloss on 'wahmiyyat' says they depend on an inner power needing intellect's testimony. The gloss on 'la yumkin an yuqni'a' explains that 'iqna' means satisfaction and 'munakara' means confrontation; the denier cannot be satisfied if he is being combative, but if he is seeking understanding, he can be satisfied if he acknowledges the shared grounds. The gloss also warns that calling sensibles 'necessary' absolutely is an error because they can err.

Correction: The passage warns that sensibles can err—for example, imagining friendship from someone who is not a friend—so they are not always necessary.

Key Takeaway
The seven certain premises form a ranked hierarchy with awwaliyat as the core, and each type has specific conditions for being considered proof, especially regarding shared experience for the last three.

Classification of Certain Premises
Classification of the remaining certain premises with reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic logicians classify certain premises beyond first principles, based on the needs of the intellect.

Core Idea
Certain premises are classified by what the intellect needs to form a judgment.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif presents a detailed classification of certain premises (muqaddimat yaqiniyya) other than awwaliyat. It shows that the intellect may require external aids, internal premises, or both, leading to distinct categories such as wahmiyyat, mushahadat, hadsiyyat, and more. Understanding this classification helps us see how different types of knowledge are grounded in reason, sense, imagination, or testimony.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Focus on the flow of classification.
Notice the repetitive structure: 'either... or...'.
2
Identify the first major division
The passage begins by dividing premises based on what the intellect needs: external aid, something to join the proposition, or both. Locate these branches.
3
Explore each subcategory
Work through each subcategory: wahmiyyat, mushahadat, qadaya fitriyya, hadsiyyat, nazariyyat, mutawatirat, and mujarrabat. Note how each is defined.
Pay attention to the role of wahm, senses, entailment, ease, reports, and repetition.
4
Understand the note on hadsiyyat
The passage explains why hadsiyyat (intuitive premises) are placed in the second section despite also needing external aids. Consider the reasoning based on reliance on the implicit syllogism.
This clarifies the criterion for classification.
5
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question that requires knowledge from this passage only.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإما أن يحتاج العقل إلى أمر ينضم إليه ويعينه في الحكم فذلك الأمر إن كان هو التوهم فهو الوهميات، وإن كان غيره فهو المشاهدات، أو يحتاج إلى أمر ينضم إلى القضية التي يحكم العقل بها ولا شك أن ذلك الأمر يكون مبادي لتلك القضية، فإن كانت لازمة فهي القضايا التي قياساتها معها، وإن كانت غير لازمة لها فإما أن يكون حصولها بسهولة فهي الحدسيات أو بصعوبة وهي النظريات وليست من المبادي الأول، أو يحتاج إليهما معاً فإما أن يكون من شأنه أن يحصل بالأخبار وهو المتواترات أو لا وهو المجربات فإن العقل فيهما يحتاج إلى أمر ينضم إليه وهو استماع الأخبار في التواتر وتكرار المشاهدات في التجربة وإلى أمر آخر ينضم إلى القضية وهو القياس الخفي، ولك أن تدرج الحدسيات في هذا القسم لاحتياجها إلى تكرار المشاهدة، والقياس الخفي معاً لكن التعويل فيها على القياس الحاصل بلا تجشم كسب فلذلك أدرجت فيما قبله . قوله : (غیره ) أي من الحواس. قوله : ( يكون مبادي إلخ ) إذ الأجنبي لا يحتاج القضية إليه . قوله : ( بسهولة) غير محتاجة إلى الحركة. قوله: (ولك أن تدرك إلخ يعني أن الحدسيات الحسية محتاجة إلى تكرار المشاهدة والعقلية الصرفة لا تحتاج إليها على ما عرفت فإن راعيت حال الحسيات منها لك أن تدرجها فيما تحتاج إليها، وإن راعيت حال العقليات أدرجتها فيما يحتاج إلى أمر ينضم إلى القضية لكن إدراجها في القسم الثاني أولى لأن التعويل على ما في الحدسيات مطلقا على القياس الخفي، ولذا لو تكرر المشاهدة في حسياتها ولم يحصل القياس لا يحصل الحكم هكذا هذا الكلام. ينبغي أن يفهم قوله : ( لاحتياجها إلى تكرار المشاهدة منع الاحتياج إليها في بعض الحدسيات قد سلف ) فلعل إدراجها فيما قبلها لذلك .
For either the intellect needs something to join it and assist it in judgment, and that thing if it is wahm [imagination] then they are wahmiyyat, and if it is other than that then they are mushahadat. Or it needs something to join the proposition that the intellect judges, and no doubt that thing will be premises for that proposition; if they are entailed then they are propositions whose syllogisms are with them; if they are not entailed, then either their attainment is easy, so they are hadsiyyat, or difficult, so they are nazariyyat and they are not from the first premises. Or it needs both together, then either it is such that it obtains by reports, so it is mutawatirat, or not, so it is mujarrabat. For the intellect in both needs something to join it, which is hearing reports in recurrence and repeating observations in experience, and another thing to join the proposition, which is the implicit syllogism. And you may include hadsiyyat in this section because they need repetition of observation and the implicit syllogism together, but reliance in them is on the syllogism obtained without effort of acquisition, so they were included in the previous section. His statement: '(other than it)' meaning from the senses. His statement: '(be premises [etc.])' because the extraneous does not need the proposition. His statement: '(easily)' not needing movement. His statement: '(and you may include [etc.])' means that sensory hadsiyyat need repetition of observation, and pure intellectual ones do not need it as you know. If you consider the situation of the sensory ones, you may include them in what needs it, and if you consider the intellectual ones, you include them in what needs something to join the proposition. But including them in the second section is preferable because reliance in hadsiyyat absolutely is on the implicit syllogism. Thus if observation is repeated in their sensory ones and the syllogism is not obtained, the judgment is not obtained. This is the gist. It should be understood that his statement: '(due to their need for repetition of observation)' denies the need for it in some hadsiyyat as already stated; perhaps their inclusion in the previous section is for that reason.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed classification of the certain premises other than awwaliyat, explaining how the intellect may need various kinds of assistance. It begins by dividing the possibilities: either the intellect needs something external to assist it in judgment; if that external is wahm (imagination), the premises are wahmiyyat; if it is something else (like perception), they are mushahadat. Or the intellect needs something to join the proposition itself—that something becomes premises for the proposition. If those premises are entailed (lazima) by the proposition, they are 'propositions whose syllogisms are with them' (i.e., qadaya fitriyya). If not entailed, then if the attainment is easy, they are hadsiyyat (intuitive); if difficult, they are nazariyyat (theoretical) and not among the first premises. Or the intellect needs both external aid and internal premises together; then if the external aid comes from reports, it is mutawatirat; otherwise it is mujarrabat. The text then notes that hadsiyyat could also be included in this combined category because they often require repetition of observation and an implicit syllogism (qiyas khafi). However, the reliance in hadsiyyat is on the implicit syllogism obtained without effort, so they are better placed in the previous category (qadaya fitriyya). The gloss clarifies each point: 'ghayrihi' means from the senses; 'mabadi' means premises; 'bi suhula' means without striving; and the discussion about including hadsiyyat reinforces that sensory hadsiyyat need repetition but intellectual ones do not, and the overall reliance is on the syllogism. This careful analysis shows the systematic reasoning behind the classification of certain premises.

المشاهدات
الحدسيات
النظريات
المتواترات
المجربات
القياس الخفي
القضايا التي قياساتها معها

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All certain premises are purely rational and require no input from senses, imagination, or testimony.
Correction: The passage shows that many certain premises depend on external aids like imagination (wahmiyyat), senses (mushahadat), reports (mutawatirat), or repeated experience (mujarrabat).

C) Hadiyyat
D) Mutawatirat

Key Takeaway
Certain premises are classified by what the intellect requires: external aid, propositional premises, or both, leading to seven distinct categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which category of certain premises do you rely on most in your daily judgments—sensory observation, widespread reports, or intuitive reasoning? Are you aware of the implicit syllogism that underlies your intuitive convictions? This week, pause before acting on an intuitive judgment and try to articulate the hidden premise.
?
The text shows that even mutawatirat and mujarrabat depend on an implicit syllogism. Have you ever accepted a claim as 'certain' without examining the reasoning behind it—perhaps from a teacher or a book? What would it take for you to verify that hidden syllogism in one area of your faith?
?
Consider a belief you hold with certainty that is neither self-evident nor based on direct observation. Using this classification, identify its category and what the intellect 'needs' for it. Are you willing to challenge whether you truly have those needs fulfilled, or have you settled for a weaker foundation?

Probable Premises: Musallamat and Mashhurat
Enumeration and clarification of probable premise types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what makes a premise probable (zanni), the first two types of probable premises (musallamat and mashhurat), and how the same proposition can be certain or probable depending on the aspect (al-'unwan).

Core Idea
A premise is probable when accepted without its ultimate proof.

This lesson introduces the key concept of probable premises in Islamic theology and jurisprudence. The author explains that probable premises are used as evidence (imara) and lists four types, though only the first two are covered here: musallamat (accepted propositions) and mashhurat (well-known propositions). A crucial clarification shows that certainty depends not on content but on the basis of belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Note the author's compact style and the gloss that follows.
The passage starts with 'And (the) probable premises...' and includes a gloss in the middle.
2
Understand the definition of probable premises
Probable premises (al-muqaddimat al-zanniyya) are used only as evidence (imara), not for certainty. The text says there are four types; we see the first two here.
The word 'imara' indicates a sign that leads to probability, not certainty.
3
Identify the first type: Musallamat
Musallamat are propositions accepted as if they were proven elsewhere. Example: principles of usul al-fiqh that a faqih accepts and uses to derive fiqh rulings, because they are proven in their proper discipline.
The acceptance is conditional: it's a placeholder until the proof is examined elsewhere.
4
Identify the second type: Mashhurat
Mashhurat are propositions agreed upon by a large multitude of people. The text gives the example 'God is one' and clarifies that if believed due to popular agreement, it is probable; if due to its decisive proof, it is certain.
This example shows that the same content can be either probable or certain based on the aspect (al-'unwan).
5
Reflect on the terminological note
The gloss defines 'probable' here as the opposite of certain, which includes mujarrabat (experiential propositions) that lack certainty. This prevents misunderstanding that all probable premises are based on mere opinion.
Mujarrabat are empirical generalizations; they can be probable if not certain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( و ) المقدمات ( الظنية ) التي تستعمل الإمارة فقط ( أربع الأولى مسلمات تقبل على أنها مبرهنة في موضع آخر) كمسائل أصول الفقه إذا سلمها الفقيه وبنى عليها الأحكام الفقهية، لكونها مبرهنة موضعها ( الثانية مشهورات اتفق عليها الجم الغفير من الناس فقد تكون قوله : ( كقولنا الإله واحد فإن قلت سياق كلامه يدل على ظنية هذه القضية مع أنها قطعية يقينية قلت : ظنيتها إنما هي إذا اعتقد بها بسبب اجتماع الجم الغفير عليها، وأما إذا لوحظت بدليلها القطعي اليقيني فهي قطعية يقينية فالاختلاف بالقطعية والظنية باختلاف العنوان، ثم اعلم أن المراد بالظنية ها هنا ما يقابل اليقينية على ما سبق هذا الاصطلاح فيشمل المجربات الخالية عن اليقين.
And (the) probable premises that are used only as evidence are (four: first are musallamat [accepted propositions] that are accepted as if they were proven elsewhere, like issues of usul al-fiqh when the faqih accepts them and builds upon them fiqh rulings, because they are proven in their proper place. Second are mashhurat [well-known propositions] agreed upon by a large multitude of people, and they may be... His statement: (like our saying 'God is one'; for if you say, the context of his words shows the probability of this proposition while it is certain and decisive, I say: its probability is only if it is believed due to the agreement of the large multitude; but if it is considered by its certain and decisive proof, it is certain and decisive. So the difference between certainty and probability is by the aspect. Then know that what is meant by probability here is what is opposite of certainty, according to the terminology that preceded; this includes mujarrabat that are devoid of certainty.)
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage introduces the category of probable premises (al-muqaddimat al-zanniyya), which are used solely as evidence (imara) and not for certainty. The main text states there are four types, but the OCR only provides the first two: musallamat and mashhurat. Musallamat are propositions accepted as if they were demonstrated elsewhere, such as principles of usul al-fiqh that a faqih accepts and uses to derive fiqh rulings, because they are proven in their proper discipline. Mashhurat are propositions agreed upon by a large multitude of people. The gloss provides an important clarification using the example 'God is one': although this is a certain proposition, when considered just by popular agreement it is probable; the certainty or probability depends on the aspect (bi-ikhtilaf al-'unwan). The gloss further explains that 'probable' here is defined as the opposite of certain, a technical term that includes even mujarrabat (experiential propositions) that lack certainty. This nuance prevents misunderstanding: the mashhurat as such are only probable, but the same content may be certain if based on a decisive proof.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Well-known propositions (mashhurat) are always certain.
Correction: Mashhurat are only probable when based on popular agreement; the same content may be certain if based on a decisive proof. The certainty or probability depends on the aspect.

Checkpoint
According to the text, why would the proposition 'God is one' be considered probable when taken as a mashhura?
A) Because it is not actually true.
B) Because it is believed due to the agreement of a large multitude, not due to its certain proof.
C) Because it is based on mujarrabat.
D) Because it is a musallama.

Key Takeaway
Probable premises are accepted not because of their own proof but due to external factors like being accepted elsewhere or popular agreement, and the same proposition can be certain or probable depending on the basis of belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever believed something simply because 'everyone says so'? What is one belief you hold that you have never examined with decisive proof? Will you commit to seeking that proof, starting today?
?
The lesson shows that the same truth (God is one) can be held as probable or certain depending on the basis. On what basis do you affirm your faith in God? Is it on the strength of proof, or social upbringing? How would your life change if you moved from probable acceptance to certainty?
?
Think of one important decision you make regularly (e.g., about your career, relationships, or worship). On what kind of premise is it based: musallamat (accepted from others) or qat'iyya (personally verified)? Are you comfortable with that? If not, what step will you take this week to ground it in certainty?

Premises Requiring External Aid: Imagination and Observation
Classifies premises that need an external aid into imaginative and observational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how premises are classified based on whether the mind needs external aid from imagination or something else.

Core Idea
Premises are classified by what aid the mind needs — imagination or other external aid.

Sanusi begins by examining the simplest case: when the mind needs help from outside. The type of premise depends on whether that external aid comes from imagination (wahm) or something else, such as sense perception. This division sets the foundation for more complex classifications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the basic division.
2
Identify the two types
Find the two types of premises: wahmiyyat (imaginative) and mushahadat (observational). Notice the condition that determines which type arises.
3
Ask: what is the external aid?
Reflect on what kind of external aid is present in each case. For wahmiyyat, it is imagination; for mushahadat, it is something else (like sensory perception).
The text does not specify what 'other than imagination' includes, but it typically includes sensory experiences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإما أن يحتاج العقل إلى أمر ينضم إليه ويعينه في الحكم فذلك الأمر إن كان هو التوهم فهو الوهميات، وإن كان غيره فهو المشاهدات، أو يحتاج إلى أمر ينضم إلى القضية التي يحكم العقل بها ولا شك أن ذلك الأمر يكون مبادي لتلك القضية، فإن كانت لازمة فهي القضايا التي قياساتها معها، وإن كانت غير لازمة لها فإما أن يكون حصولها بسهولة فهي الحدسيات أو بصعوبة وهي النظريات وليست من المبادي الأول، أو يحتاج إليهما معاً فإما أن يكون من شأنه أن يحصل بالأخبار وهو المتواترات أو لا وهو المجربات فإن العقل فيهما يحتاج إلى أمر ينضم إليه وهو استماع الأخبار في التواتر وتكرار المشاهدات في التجربة وإلى أمر آخر ينضم إلى القضية وهو القياس الخفي، ولك أن تدرج الحدسيات في هذا القسم لاحتياجها إلى تكرار المشاهدة، والقياس الخفي معاً لكن التعويل فيها على القياس الحاصل بلا تجشم كسب فلذلك أدرجت فيما قبله.
For either the mind needs something to join it and assist it in judgment, then that thing, if it is imagination, then it is imaginative premises; and if it is other than it, then it is observational premises. Or it needs something to join the proposition that the mind judges, and no doubt that thing is a starting point for that proposition. If it is necessary, then they are propositions whose syllogisms are with them; and if they are not necessary for it, then either their attainment is easy, then they are intuitive premises, or with difficulty, and they are theoretical premises, and they are not from the first starting points. Or it needs both together, then either it is of such nature that it is obtained by reports, and they are recurrent reports, or not, and they are experimental premises. For the mind in both needs something to join it, which is hearing reports in recurrence and repetition of observations in experiment, and another thing to join the proposition, which is the hidden syllogism. And you may include intuitive premises in this section due to their need for repetition of observation and the hidden syllogism together, but reliance in them is on the syllogism obtained without striving for acquisition, therefore they were included in the previous section.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage classifies premises (muqaddimat) according to what the mind needs to make a judgment. It is a definition-and-enumeration passage. The author first considers two situations: (1) The mind needs an external aid to judge. If that aid is imagination (wahm), the premises are wahmiyyat; if it is something else (e.g., sense perception), they are mushahadat (observational). (2) The mind needs something that attaches to the proposition itself, which serves as a starting point (mabda'). If the proposition is necessary (i.e., the conclusion follows necessarily), then the premises are 'qadaya qiyasatuha ma'aha' (propositions whose syllogisms are with them). If the proposition is not necessary, then if its attainment is easy, it is 'hidsiyyat' (intuitive); if difficult, it is 'nazariyyat' (theoretical) – these are not first principles. Then a third case: the mind needs both an external aid and an aid attached to the proposition. The external aid may come from reports (tawatur) or repetition of observation (tajriba) – these are 'mutawatirat' (recurrent) and 'mujarrabat' (experimental). Additionally, there is a hidden syllogism (qiyas khafi) that attaches to the proposition. The author notes that intuitive premises could be included in this third section because they require repetition of observation and hidden syllogism, but since the reliance is on the syllogism without effort, they are better placed in the earlier section.

Key Terms
الوهميات
المشاهدات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Observational premises (mushahadat) only come from sense perception.
Correction: Mushahadat include any external aid other than imagination, which may include sense perception but also other types of external inputs.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what determines whether a premise is wahmiyyat or mushahadat?
A) Whether the premise is based on reports
B) Whether the external aid is imagination or something else
C) Whether the premise requires a syllogism
D) Whether the premise is easy to attain

Key Takeaway
The first division of premises is based on whether the external aid to the mind is imagination or something else, yielding wahmiyyat and mushahadat respectively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a conviction about your faith, have you ever stopped to ask whether it comes from a reliable source or from your own imagination? How would your certainty change if you discovered it was built on wahm rather than mushahadah?
?
Your mind constantly uses external aids—from your senses to your assumptions. Which of your deeply held beliefs might actually be resting on imagination, not observation? What would it take for you to test them against the sources?
?
If this classification is true, then admitting that some of your premises are merely wahmiyyat requires humility. Are you willing to revisit a belief you've never questioned, even if it feels uncomfortable?

Premises Needing an Attached Starting Point: Intuitive and Theoretical
Classifies premises needing an attached starting point into self-evident, intuitive, and theoretical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how premises are classified when the mind needs a starting point attached to the proposition, leading to self-evident, intuitive, or theoretical premises.

Core Idea
Premises are self-evident, intuitive, or theoretical based on their necessity and ease of attainment.

Sanusi now considers premises where the mind does not need external aid but rather something attached to the proposition itself—a starting point. He divides these into three subtypes: those where the conclusion follows necessarily (self-evident), those attained easily (intuitive), and those attained with difficulty (theoretical).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation to understand the three subcategories.
2
Identify the three categories
Find the categories: qadaya qiyasatuha ma'aha (self-evident), hidsiyyat (intuitive), and nazariyyat (theoretical). Note their defining features.
3
Understand the 'not first principles' note
Notice that nazariyyat are explicitly said not to be from the first starting points (mabadi' awal). This is important to avoid confusion.

Premises Requiring Both External and Internal Aids: Recurrent, Experimental, and Hidden Syllogism
Classifies premises needing both external and internal aids into recurrent and experimental, with a note on the hidden syllogism and intuitive premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how premises that need both an external aid and an attached starting point are classified into recurrent reports, experimental premises, and the role of the hidden syllogism, along with a note on intuitive premises.

Core Idea
Some premises need both an external aid (reports or observations) and an internal syllogism.

Sanusi now tackles the most complex case: premises that require both an external aid and an internal starting point attached to the proposition. He distinguishes between recurrent reports (mutawatirat) and experimental premises (mujarrabat), both of which also involve a hidden syllogism (qiyas khafi). He then clarifies why intuitive premises (hidsiyyat) were not placed in this category despite sharing some features.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

2
Identify the two types: mutawatirat and mujarrabat
Find the definitions: mutawatirat come from reports, mujarrabat from repeated observations. Notice both also require a hidden syllogism.
3
Understand the note on hidsiyyat
The author explains why hidsiyyat could be included here but are placed earlier: because their reliance is on the effortless syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Four Types of Probable Premises
Enumerates and defines the four types of probable premises used in dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of probable premises used in dialectical argumentation and their distinguishing features.

Core Idea
Probable premises are signs, not certain proofs, and come in four categories.

In dialectical reasoning, not all premises are certain. The author classifies probable (zanni) premises into four types, each with its own basis of acceptance. This lesson introduces these categories, showing how they function as indicators ('imara) rather than definitive proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to get the full classification.
Notice the author's systematic enumeration.
2
Identify the four categories
Find the four types: musallamat, mashhurat, maqbulat, muqarana bi al-qara'in. Note the Arabic names.
3
Examine the definitions and examples
For each type, understand its definition and the example given. See how they differ.
Pay attention to the special case of maqbulat vs. prophetic reports.
4
Consider the misconception
Reflect on the common misunderstanding that mashhurat are always true. The author explicitly says they can be false.
This is a key nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقدمات الظنية التي تستعمل الإمارة فقط أربع: الأولى مسلمات تقبل على أنها مبرهنة في موضع آخر كمسائل أصول الفقه إذا سلمها الفقيه وبنى عليها الأحكام الفقهية لكونها مبرهنة موضعها. الثانية مشهورات اتفق عليها الجم الغفير من الناس فقد تكون مشهورة عند الكل كقولنا العدل حسن والظلم قبيح أو عند الأكثر كقولنا الإله واحد، أو عند طائفة كقولنا التسلسل مطلقاً محال. وبالجملة فالمشهورات ما يحكم بها لتطابق الآراء عليها إما لمصلحة عامة أو رقة أو حمية أو تأديبات شرعية أو انفعالات خلقية أو مزاجية، سواء كانت صادقة أو كاذبة. الثالثة مقبولات تؤخذ ممن حسن الظن فيه أنه لا يكذب كالمأخوذات من العلماء الأخيار والحكماء الأبرار، بخلاف المأخوذات من الأنبياء الذين علم أنهم لا يكذبون، فإنها بعد ما علم استنادها إليهم مستعملة في الأدلة النقلية كما ستعرفها. الرابعة المقرونة بالقرائن كنزول المطر لوجود السحاب الرطب.
And the probable premises that are used only as an indication are four: First, accepted truths (musallamat) that are accepted as being demonstrated in another place, like questions of usul al-fiqh when the jurist accepts them and builds upon them legal rulings, because they are demonstrated in their proper place. Second, well-known opinions (mashhurat) agreed upon by a great multitude of people. They may be well-known among all, like our saying 'justice is good and injustice is evil,' or among the majority, like our saying 'God is one,' or among a group, like our saying 'absolute infinite regress is impossible.' In summary, well-known opinions are those judged by agreement of opinions upon them, either for a general interest, or tenderness, or zeal, or religious disciplines, or moral or temperamental emotions, whether true or false. Third, accepted sayings (maqbulat) taken from one whom it is good opinion about him that he does not lie, like what is taken from good scholars and pious sages, unlike what is taken from prophets who are known that they do not lie, for after it is known that it is based on them, it is used in transmitted proofs as you will know. Fourth, those accompanied by indications (muqarana bi al-qara'in), like the descent of rain by the presence of wet clouds.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage enumerates and defines the four types of probable (zanni) premises used in dialectical argumentation. It is a classification passage. The author lists: (1) Musallamat – propositions accepted as true because they are proven elsewhere, even if not proven here. Example: principles of usul al-fiqh. (2) Mashhurat – well-known opinions widely agreed upon. They can be universal (e.g., 'justice is good'), majority (e.g., 'God is one'), or group-specific (e.g., 'infinite regress is impossible'). Agreement may arise from various motives like public interest, compassion, zeal, religious training, or emotional disposition, regardless of truth. (3) Maqbulat – statements taken from trustworthy authorities (e.g., scholars) but not prophets, as prophetic reports are certain and belong to transmitted proofs. (4) Muqarana bi al-qara'in – premises accompanied by external indications, like inferring rain from wet clouds. The author emphasizes these are only used as signs ('imara'), not certain proofs.

Key Terms
المقدمات الظنية
مسلمات
مشهورات
مقبولات
المقرونة بالقرائن
الإمارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That well-known opinions (mashhurat) are always true because many people agree.
Correction: The author explicitly states that mashhurat can be false; they are based on agreement which may stem from non-truth-related motives.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between maqbulat and prophetic reports?
A) Maqbulat are based on consensus, prophetic reports on individual authority.
B) Prophetic reports are certain because prophets are known not to lie, while maqbulat are only probable.
C) Maqbulat are used in dialectic, prophetic reports in transmitted proofs.
D) There is no difference.

Key Takeaway
Probable premises are categorised into four types based on their source of acceptance, but all are merely indications, not certain proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accept a common belief just because 'everyone says so,' you are relying on a mashhura that may be false. Which widely held belief have you never questioned, and what would it cost you to examine it critically today?
?
In your daily decisions, how often do you treat probable indications (imara) as if they were certain? Identify one such belief you hold based on signs, and commit to acknowledging its uncertainty before acting on it.
?
The scholars who offered maqbulat are trusted for their character. Whose opinion do you trust without verifying? Is that trust based on their knowledge or your comfort? Would you still follow them if you knew their claim was only probable?

First Premise: Numbers and Infinite Regress
Analysis of the first premise on numbers and its consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first premise regarding the similarity of numbers, its problematic implication of infinite regress, and the key objection that distinguishes between finite and infinite numbers based on decisive proof.

Core Idea
The similarity of numbers leads to an unwanted infinite regress, but a decisive proof saves the argument.

This lesson introduces the first premise often used in arguments about the world's temporal origin. The author presents that numbers are essentially similar, which leads to a consequence that opponents find unacceptable. He then qualifies this premise by addressing a specific objection concerning the difference between finite and infinite numbers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice the statement that numbers are essentially similar. This is the starting point.
The passage is dense; read the translation alongside.
2
Understand the consequence
The author argues that if all times are equal, then one can advance an event's occurrence by any number of times, leading to an infinite regress. Opponents reject this.
This shows the weakness of the premise.
3
Grasp the objection for negation
The author specifies an objection for those who deny infinite numbers: if infinite is impossible due to proof, finite cannot be analogized; if not impossible, the objection is invalid.
This is a logical nuance about the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأعداد متماثلة أصلاً ، ولزمه في صورة الاستدلال على إثبات ما لا يتناهى من فساد آخر أشار إليه بقوله : ( وإذا يلزمهم صحة عدم العالم ) فإنه يصح تقديم إحداثه من على الوقت الذي حدث فيه بوقت واحد وبوقتين وبأوقات ثلاثة وهلم جرا، لأن الأوقات كلها متساوية فيلزم صحة تقديم إحداثه على ذلك الوقت بأوقات لا نهاية لها، مع أنهم لا يقولون بها وهذا الذي ذكرناه من ضعف المقدمة الأولى مشترك بين جانبي النفي والإثبات كما تحققته ويخص جانب النفي بسؤال وهو أن ما لا يتناهي) من الأعداد ( إن امتنع لدليل قاطع دل عليه (لم يقس عليه ما لا يمتنع ) الأعداد المتناهية إذ ليس يلزم من تجويز ما لا دليل على امتناعه تجویز ما قام الدليل على امتناعه ) وإلا ) أي وإن لم يمتنع ما لا يتناهى من الأعداد لدليل دل عليه (لم يمكن نفيه ودعوى استحالته، فلا يكون اللازم من إثبات عدد مخصوص أمراً محالاً فلا يتم الاستدلال
قوله : ( وإذا يلزمهم أي إن استدل على نفي الأولوية بالتماثل يلزمهم صحة قدم العالم فهو معطوف على قوله : فإن قال : حكم الشيء إلخ عطف الشرطية على الشرطية . قوله : ( وإذا يلزمهم) عطف من حيث المعنى على مدخول مع في قوله : مع أنه لزمه لأنه في قوة قولنا : وهذا استدلال باطل لأنه لزمه نفي الواحد، ولأنهم يلزمهم صحة قدم العالم، ويجوز أن يكون معطوفاً على مدخول فيقول : من حيث المعنى أيضاً. قوله : (إذ ليس يلزم من تجويز (إلخ فإن قلت : إن سلم عدم أولوية عدد من عدد فاللزوم ظاهر، وإلا فالسؤال ما سبق لا هذا قلت : هذا منع عدم الأولوية بطريق آخر ، وهو أن ما امتنع لقاطع أولى بالعدم .
Numbers are essentially similar, and it follows in the form of inference to prove the infinite another corruption he indicated by his statement: (And if they are compelled to the soundness of the non-existence of the world) for it is valid to advance its occurrence from the time it occurred by one time and by two times and by three times and so on, because all times are equal, so the soundness of advancing its occurrence to that time by infinite times follows, while they do not affirm this. And this which we mentioned of the weakness of the first premise is shared between the sides of negation and affirmation as you have realized, and it specifically concerns the side of negation with a question: that what is infinite from numbers, if it is impossible due to a decisive proof, then what is not impossible (i.e., finite numbers) is not analogized to it, for it does not follow from permitting what has no proof of its impossibility the permitting of what has proof of its impossibility. Otherwise, i.e., if the infinite in numbers is not impossible due to a proof, then it cannot be negated or claimed to be impossible, so the necessary consequent from establishing a particular number is not an impossibility, so the inference is not complete. He said: (And if they are compelled) i.e., if they infer by denying priority through similarity, they are compelled to the soundness of the pre-eternity of the world, so it is conjoined with his statement: 'If it is said: the judgment of the thing etc.' as a conjunction of a conditional with a conditional. He said: (And if they are compelled) is conjoined in meaning with the object of 'with' in his statement: 'with that it follows from him' because it is in the force of our saying: 'This is an invalid inference because it entails the negation of the One, and because they are compelled to the soundness of the pre-eternity of the world.' And it is permissible that it be conjoined with the object of 'in' in his statement, meaning also. He said: (For it does not follow from permitting etc.) For if you say: If the non-priority of one number over another is granted, then the implication is apparent; otherwise, the question is the previous one, not this. I say: This is a denial of non-priority by another way, namely that what is impossible due to a decisive proof is more worthy of non-existence.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the first premise concerning numbers and the problem of infinite regress. It begins by stating that numbers are essentially similar, and then presents a consequence: if one allows the possibility of advancing the occurrence of an event by one time, two times, etc., due to the equality of all times, then it would be possible to advance it by an infinite number of times, which the opponent (likely the Mu'tazila or philosophers) does not accept. This shows the weakness of the first premise. The author then specifies that this weakness applies to both the negative and positive sides, but he addresses the negative side with a specific objection: if infinite numbers are impossible due to a decisive proof, then finite numbers cannot be analogized to them; if they are not impossible, then one cannot simply claim they are impossible, and so the inference fails. The commentary (قوله) further explains the logical connections, noting that the argument if one infers from similarity leads to the pre-eternity of the world, which is a problem. The commentator also addresses a potential question: if the non-priority of numbers is granted, the implication is clear; otherwise, the question is old. The response is that this is a different denial of non-priority, stating that what is impossible due to a decisive proof is more worthy of non-existence. The passage is highly technical, dealing with the logic of infinite regress and its implications for arguments about the world's temporal origin.

Key Terms
الأعداد
ما لا يتناهى
المقدمة
التماثل
الاستدلال
القدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the similarity of numbers is a mathematical fact about equality of numbers.
Correction: It is a logical premise used to argue about infinite regress in time, not a mathematical claim.

Checkpoint
What is the main consequence drawn from the equality of all times in the first premise?
A) That numbers are essentially different
B) That advancing an event by infinite times is possible
C) That the world is pre-eternal
D) That the infinite is impossible

Key Takeaway
The first premise about the similarity of numbers leads to an unwanted infinite regress, which is only avoided by a decisive proof that distinguishes finite from infinite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on logical arguments to affirm your faith, do you ever assume that all premises are equally valid, ignoring the need for a decisive proof? What assumption in your life today might lead to an infinite regress of excuses? Commit to examining one foundation of your belief with the same rigor as the author demands.
?
If the similarity of numbers can lead to an unacceptable infinite regress, what seemingly harmless assumptions in your daily choices are delaying critical decisions? Where in your life are you failing to apply a 'decisive proof' to break the chain of procrastination? Identify one action you will take within the next 24 hours.
?
The author distinguishes between what is impossible by proof and what is merely not proven impossible. Are you dismissing truths about your own soul because you lack a decisive proof, or are you accepting falsehoods because no one has proven them impossible? What will you investigate today with the sincerity of a seeker of certainty?

Why Transmission Cannot Prove Its Own Foundations
Defines the second type and explains the circularity argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of God, His attributes, and prophethood must be known rationally to avoid circularity.

Core Idea
Revelation cannot prove its own foundations without circularity.

The commentator presents a crucial argument in Islamic theology: the proof of revelation itself depends on rational knowledge of God and prophethood. Otherwise, we would be caught in circular reasoning. This passage explains the chain of dependencies and why rational proof is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the second type
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that it defines the second type of demand as 'what transmission depends on'.
2
Trace the dependency chain
Follow the chain: soundness of transmission → truthfulness of informant → prophethood proven by miracle → existence and attributes of God. Understand how each link depends on the previous.
The verse from Ibrahim is cited as support for God's choice.
3
Understand why circularity is avoided
Reflect: if transmission were used to prove these foundations, each would depend on the other (circularity). Therefore, reason must establish them independently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني من المطالب ما يتوقف عليه النقل مثل وجود الصانع وكونه عالماً قادراً مختاراً ونبوة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم فهذا المطلوب لا يثبت إلا بالعقل إذ لو ثبت بالنقل لزم الدور لأن كل واحد منهما يتوقف حينئذ على الآخر. قوله : (مثل وجود إلخ ) فإن صحة النقل تتوقف على صدق المخبر وهو يتوقف على ثبوت نبوته بإظهار المعجزة في يده وهو يتوقف على وجود الصانع، وكونه عالما حتى يخلق المعجزة على وفق دعواه، وكونه قادراً على خلق المعجزة، وكونه مريداً يختار لمن يشاء من عباده بالنبوة على ما نطق به قوله تعالى ولكن الله يمن على من يشاء من عباده ﴾ [إبراهيم: ١١ ]. ( نزاع في كونه عقلياً فإن العصيان في اللغة ضد الطاعة، فلو أمر أحد غيره ولم يمتثل ذلك الغير لأمره يعد ذلك الغير الممتثل عاصياً، وإن لم يكن الأمر شارعاً، وقد يراد به استحقاق العقاب فهو حينئذ شرعي، فبالنظر إلى الأول عد صاحب المقاصد قولنا كل واجب فتاركه عاص، مقدمة عقلية وبالنظر إلى الثاني عد الشارح قوله : وكل تارك المأمور به عاص مقدمة شرعية لا عقلية من المعنى الأول كما توهم بل لأنه لو حمل عليه للغا الحمل، إذ يكون المؤدى تارك المأمور به تارك المأمور به اللهم إلا أن يفرق بين ترك المأمور به وترك نفس امتثال الأمر وإن تلازما.
The second of the demands is what transmission depends on, such as the existence of the Maker, His being knowing, powerful, and willing, and the prophethood of Muhammad, peace be upon him. So this demand is not established except by reason, for if it were established by transmission, circularity would follow, because each of them would then depend on the other. His statement: “Like existence, etc.” for the soundness of transmission depends on the truthfulness of the informant, and that depends on the establishment of his prophethood by the manifestation of a miracle at his hand, and that depends on the existence of the Maker, His being knowing so that He creates the miracle in accordance with his claim, His being powerful to create the miracle, and His being willing who chooses for whom He wills from His servants prophethood, as is stated by His saying, may He be exalted: “But Allah selects for His mercy whom He wills” [Ibrahim 11]. (A dispute concerning it being rational: for disobedience in language is the opposite of obedience. So if someone commands another and that other does not comply with his command, that non-complying other is considered disobedient, even if the commander is not a legislator. And it may be intended to mean deserving punishment, in which case it is legal. So with regard to the first, the author of al-Maqasid counted our statement “every obligatory act, its abandoner is disobedient” as a rational premise; and with regard to the second, the commentator counted his statement “and every abandoner of the commanded act is disobedient” as a legal premise, not rational from the first meaning as was imagined. Rather, because if it were carried upon it, the carrying would be futile, since the content would be “abandoner of the commanded act is an abandoner of the commanded act.” Unless a distinction is made between abandoning the commanded act and abandoning the very compliance with the command, even if they entail each other.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of demand (matlab): what transmission itself depends on, such as the existence of God, His attributes of knowledge, power, and will, and the prophethood of Muhammad. These cannot be proven by transmission because that would lead to circularity—each (transmission and these foundations) would depend on the other. The commentator explains the dependency chain: the soundness of transmission requires a truthful informant, whose prophethood is proven by miracles, which in turn require a powerful, knowing, and willing God. The text then addresses a dispute over whether the premise 'every abandoner of an obligatory act is disobedient' is rational or legal. The commentator clarifies that 'disobedience' has two meanings: linguistic (non-compliance) and legal (deserving punishment). The premise is rational in the first sense and legal in the second. If taken as rational in the legal sense, it would be tautological. A distinction is offered between abandoning the act and abandoning compliance, though they entail each other.

Key Terms
الدور (al-dawr: circularity)
صحة النقل (ṣiḥḥat al-naql: soundness of transmission)
المعجزة (al-mu'jiza: miracle)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All religious knowledge comes from revelation, including the existence of God.
Correction: This passage shows that the foundations of revelation itself must be known rationally to avoid circularity.

Checkpoint
Why can the existence of the Maker not be established by transmitted proof according to this passage?
A) Because it is rationally impossible
B) Because it would lead to circularity
C) Because it is self-evident
D) Because the Qur'an does not mention it

Key Takeaway
The foundations of transmission must be known rationally to avoid circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you rely on reason to confirm the foundations of your faith, or have you accepted them without rational verification? What might be at stake if you haven’t?
?
If someone asks you why you believe in God, do you instinctively point to the Quran or hadith first? After this lesson, how might you answer differently to avoid circular reasoning?
?
The passage says transmission depends on a truthful prophet, which depends on miracles, which depend on God. Have you ever thought through this chain for yourself? If not, what holds you back from doing so—and will you commit to thinking it through today?

Is 'Disobedience' a Rational or Legal Premise?
Discusses a dispute over whether a premise is rational or legal based on the meaning of 'disobedience'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the meaning of 'disobedience' determines whether a premise is rational or legal.

Core Idea
'Disobedience' has two meanings, and only one yields a rational premise.

A subtle dispute arises over whether a common theological premise—'every abandoner of an obligatory act is disobedient'—is based on reason or revelation. The commentator clarifies by distinguishing two senses of the key term 'disobedience'.

Guided Path
1
Read the dispute passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice it begins with 'نزاع في كونه عقليا' (a dispute concerning it being rational).
2
Identify the two meanings of 'disobedience'
The commentator explains that 'disobedience' (العصيان) has a linguistic meaning (non-compliance) and a legal meaning (deserving punishment). Note how each leads to a different classification of the premise.
3
Understand the consequence of confusing the meanings
If one takes the premise with the legal meaning but claims it is rational, it becomes tautological (abandoner of the commanded act = abandoner of the commanded act). Hence the correct classification depends on the intended meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني من المطالب ما يتوقف عليه النقل مثل وجود الصانع وكونه عالماً قادراً مختاراً ونبوة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم فهذا المطلوب لا يثبت إلا بالعقل إذ لو ثبت بالنقل لزم الدور لأن كل واحد منهما يتوقف حينئذ على الآخر. قوله : (مثل وجود إلخ ) فإن صحة النقل تتوقف على صدق المخبر وهو يتوقف على ثبوت نبوته بإظهار المعجزة في يده وهو يتوقف على وجود الصانع، وكونه عالما حتى يخلق المعجزة على وفق دعواه، وكونه قادراً على خلق المعجزة، وكونه مريداً يختار لمن يشاء من عباده بالنبوة على ما نطق به قوله تعالى ولكن الله يمن على من يشاء من عباده ﴾ [إبراهيم: ١١ ]. ( نزاع في كونه عقلياً فإن العصيان في اللغة ضد الطاعة، فلو أمر أحد غيره ولم يمتثل ذلك الغير لأمره يعد ذلك الغير الممتثل عاصياً، وإن لم يكن الأمر شارعاً، وقد يراد به استحقاق العقاب فهو حينئذ شرعي، فبالنظر إلى الأول عد صاحب المقاصد قولنا كل واجب فتاركه عاص، مقدمة عقلية وبالنظر إلى الثاني عد الشارح قوله : وكل تارك المأمور به عاص مقدمة شرعية لا عقلية من المعنى الأول كما توهم بل لأنه لو حمل عليه للغا الحمل، إذ يكون المؤدى تارك المأمور به تارك المأمور به اللهم إلا أن يفرق بين ترك المأمور به وترك نفس امتثال الأمر وإن تلازما.
The second of the demands is what transmission depends on, such as the existence of the Maker, His being knowing, powerful, and willing, and the prophethood of Muhammad, peace be upon him. So this demand is not established except by reason, for if it were established by transmission, circularity would follow, because each of them would then depend on the other. His statement: “Like existence, etc.” for the soundness of transmission depends on the truthfulness of the informant, and that depends on the establishment of his prophethood by the manifestation of a miracle at his hand, and that depends on the existence of the Maker, His being knowing so that He creates the miracle in accordance with his claim, His being powerful to create the miracle, and His being willing who chooses for whom He wills from His servants prophethood, as is stated by His saying, may He be exalted: “But Allah selects for His mercy whom He wills” [Ibrahim 11]. (A dispute concerning it being rational: for disobedience in language is the opposite of obedience. So if someone commands another and that other does not comply with his command, that non-complying other is considered disobedient, even if the commander is not a legislator. And it may be intended to mean deserving punishment, in which case it is legal. So with regard to the first, the author of al-Maqasid counted our statement “every obligatory act, its abandoner is disobedient” as a rational premise; and with regard to the second, the commentator counted his statement “and every abandoner of the commanded act is disobedient” as a legal premise, not rational from the first meaning as was imagined. Rather, because if it were carried upon it, the carrying would be futile, since the content would be “abandoner of the commanded act is an abandoner of the commanded act.” Unless a distinction is made between abandoning the commanded act and abandoning the very compliance with the command, even if they entail each other.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of demand (matlab): what transmission itself depends on, such as the existence of God, His attributes of knowledge, power, and will, and the prophethood of Muhammad. These cannot be proven by transmission because that would lead to circularity—each (transmission and these foundations) would depend on the other. The commentator explains the dependency chain: the soundness of transmission requires a truthful informant, whose prophethood is proven by miracles, which in turn require a powerful, knowing, and willing God. The text then addresses a dispute over whether the premise 'every abandoner of an obligatory act is disobedient' is rational or legal. The commentator clarifies that 'disobedience' has two meanings: linguistic (non-compliance) and legal (deserving punishment). The premise is rational in the first sense and legal in the second. If taken as rational in the legal sense, it would be tautological. A distinction is offered between abandoning the act and abandoning compliance, though they entail each other.

Key Terms
العصيان (al-'iṣyān: disobedience)
المقدمة العقلية (al-muqaddima al-'aqliyya: rational premise)
المقدمة الشرعية (al-muqaddima al-shar'iyya: legal premise)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The premise 'every abandoner of an obligatory act is disobedient' is purely rational.
Correction: It is rational only if 'disobedience' means linguistic non-compliance; if it means deserving punishment, it is a legal premise.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if the legal meaning of 'disobedience' is taken as a rational premise?
A) It becomes a valid rational proof
B) It becomes tautological
C) It becomes contradictory
D) It becomes impossible

Key Takeaway
The classification of a premise as rational or legal depends on the precise meaning of its terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used a word like 'obedience' or 'disobedience' in your relationship with Allah without pausing to consider what it truly means? What hidden assumptions might you be carrying that reduce your worship to mere outward actions? This lesson forces you to define your terms carefully—so, what does 'disobedience' actually mean for your daily choices? Will you now examine your definitions before you next justify a sin or a good deed?
?
The commentator warns that misusing the meaning of 'disobedience' makes the premise empty and tautological. In your own life, have you ever found yourself using religious language that sounds correct but actually says nothing—like claiming 'I am disobedient' without feeling the weight of deserving punishment? How can you ensure your self-assessment is grounded in the precise, life-altering truth of the Sharia meaning, not just a linguistic label?
?
When you hear someone argue that all humans are naturally disobedient, do you accept it as a rational statement? This lesson shows that the classification depends on the meaning of 'disobedience'. Could your casual acceptance of such statements be hiding a tautology that dulls your sense of accountability? Take a moment: Define 'disobedience' as you truly believe it before Allah, and ask yourself if that definition drives you to repentance or merely restates the obvious.

Why Transmitted Proofs Lack Certainty in Intellectual Matters
Sanusi defines shar'iyat and aqliyat to explain why transmitted proofs lack certainty in aqliyat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why transmitted proofs cannot yield certainty in intellectual matters, based on the distinction between shar'iyat and aqliyat and the role of the contextual indicator (qarina).

Core Idea
Transmitted proofs lack certainty in intellectual matters because the subject could be impossible, and no contextual indicator removes that doubt.

Sanusi tackles a key objection: if the speaker is truthful, why don't transmitted proofs give certainty in all areas? He answers by distinguishing religious matters, where the content is possible but unknown, from intellectual matters, where the content may be impossible. This distinction shows why a contextual indicator is present in one but absent in the other.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the opening sentence carefully. Sanusi states that transmitted proofs by themselves, due to the involvement of a contextual indicator, do not yield certainty in intellectual matters.
Notice the phrase 'مجردها' (by themselves).
2
Understand the objection
The objection says: if the speaker's truthfulness is certain, then certainty of no contrary should follow in both intellectual and religious matters. See if you can follow the logic.
The objection is trying to prove that there is no difference between the two domains.
3
Follow the answer and the key distinction
Sanusi defines 'religious matters' (shar'iyat) as matters whose possibility is known but reason has no path to them, and 'intellectual matters' (aqliyat) as anything else. Because intellectual matters could be impossible, that possibility prevents certainty, and there is no contextual indicator to remove it.
The conclusion is that transmitted proofs lack the necessary qarina in intellectual matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الثامن : الدلائل النقلية هل تفيد اليقين فلا جرم بمجردها، ومدخلية القرينة فيه مما لا يمكن الجزم بأحد طرفيه أي النفي والإثبات كانت إفادتها اليقين في العقليات محل نظر، وتأمل فإن قلت : إن كان صدق القائل مجزوماً به لزم منه الجزم بعدم المعارض في العقليات كما لزم منه ذلك في الشرعيات وإلا احتمل كلامه الكذب فيهما فلا فرق بينهما، قلت: المراد بالشرعيات أمور يجزم العقل بإمكانها ثبوتاً وانتفاء ولا طريق له إليها، والمراد بالعقليات ما ليس كذلك، وحينئذ جاز أن يكون من الممتنعات فلأجل هذا الاحتمال ربما لم يحصل الجزم بعدم المعارض العقلي للدليل النقلي في العقليات، وإن حصل الجزم به في الشرعيات وذلك بخلاف الأدلة العقلية في العقليات، فإنها بمجردها أقوى القرائن فالحاصل أنه إذا كان للقرينة مدخل في حصول الجزم بعدم المعارض لا يوجد في العقليات قرينة كذلك إذ من جملة القرائن الدالة على عدم الإرادة كونه من الممتنعات وهو يحتمل في العقليات كلها
The Sixth Observatory - The Eighth Goal: Do transmitted proofs yield certainty? Undoubtedly, by themselves alone, and the involvement of a contextual indicator (qarina) in it [is such that] it is not possible to be certain of one of its two sides, i.e., negation and affirmation. Their yielding certainty in intellectual matters (al-`aqliyat) is a matter for consideration and reflection. For if you say: If the truthfulness of the speaker is certain, then certainty of the absence of a mental contrary follows in intellectual matters just as it follows in religious matters (al-shar`iyat); otherwise his speech would be liable to falsehood in both, so there is no difference between them. I say: What is meant by religious matters are matters whose existence and non-existence the intellect judges to be possible, but it has no path to them. What is meant by intellectual matters is not like that. In that case, it is possible that [the matter] is among the impossible things. Because of this possibility, perhaps certainty of the absence of a mental contrary to the transmitted proof in intellectual matters is not obtained, even if certainty of it is obtained in religious matters. And that is contrary to intellectual proofs in intellectual matters, for they by themselves are the strongest of contextual indicators. The conclusion: If a contextual indicator has an entry in obtaining certainty of the absence of a contrary, there is no such contextual indicator in intellectual matters, for among the contextual indicators indicating the lack of intention is that it is among the impossible things, and that is possible in all intellectual matters.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage defines the problem of whether transmitted proofs (adilla naqliyya) yield certainty (yaqin) in intellectual matters. The author begins by stating that transmitted proofs by themselves, given the involvement of a contextual indicator (qarina), cannot lead to certainty in intellectual matters because one cannot be certain whether the indicator points to negation or affirmation. He then presents an objection: if the speaker (e.g., God or the Prophet) is known to be truthful, then certainty of no mental contrary should follow in both religious and intellectual matters. The answer distinguishes religious matters (shar`iyat) as those whose possibility the intellect knows but has no independent path to, while intellectual matters are not so limited; they may involve impossibilities that override the transmitted proof. Thus, transmitted proofs in intellectual matters lack the contextual indicator needed for certainty, unlike intellectual proofs which are the strongest indicators themselves.

Key Terms
الدلائل النقلية
اليقين
القرينة
العقليات
الشرعيات
الممتنعات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the speaker (God or the Prophet) is truthful, transmitted proofs should yield certainty in all matters equally.
Correction: Certainty also depends on the subject matter. In intellectual matters, the possibility that the content is impossible prevents certainty, even if the speaker is truthful.

Checkpoint
Why do transmitted proofs not yield certainty in intellectual matters according to the definition given?
A) Because the speaker might be lying
B) Because intellectual matters are impossible for the mind to grasp
C) Because intellectual matters could be impossible, and no contextual indicator removes that possibility
D) Because religious matters are always known through reason

Key Takeaway
Transmitted proofs do not yield certainty in intellectual matters because the possibility that the subject is impossible prevents any contextual indicator from removing doubt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How many of the core beliefs you hold about God are based solely on transmitted proofs without the rational certainty required for intellectual matters? Are you willing to examine those beliefs rationally, or do you avoid the discomfort of uncertainty?
?
If the mere possibility that a matter could be impossible prevents certainty from transmitted proofs, what does that mean for the strength of your conviction in doctrines like the attributes of God? Have you ever considered that some of your beliefs might be based on tradition rather than rational demonstration?
?
This lesson challenges the sufficiency of transmitted proofs for intellectual matters. In what areas of your faith have you relied on 'because God said so' without seeking rational grounding? What would it cost you to deepen your understanding through rational arguments?
